# 证道一贯真机易简录

Here is the translation:

---

**Preface**

Long ago, I held that the golden tablets and jade scrolls, the Fire Dragon and Water Tiger—texts of utmost truth—resided in celestial grotto-heavens and the stone chambers of sacred mountains, to be encountered only when fate aligned. Little did I know that the celestial mechanism is not inscribed on bamboo or silk, and the supreme Dao abides among men.

I once believed that the golden elixir and jade essence, true yin and true yang, the most wondrous medicine, must conceal themselves in heaven as heaven and in earth as earth, beyond conception, elusive as shadows and echoes. Little did I know that they lie not in distant realms but right before one’s eyes.

When the First Emperor of Qin journeyed east to Mount Tai, he sent boys and girls into the sea in search of the elixir of immortality—unaware that the medicine was not in the sea but in the mountains. Emperor Wu of Han erected dew basins and celestial palms, consuming the ethereal *hanglong* and golden nectar—unaware that the nectar was not in the heavens but upon the earth. As the saying goes, *“Flowers bloom everywhere, never withering,”* and again, *“When they bloom across the land, the River of Love is crossed.”* This is proof enough.

Since the Most High bestowed the transmission, the lineage of the Dao has never been severed, and its burden rests upon humanity. Heart transmits to heart, mouth to mouth. Without the slightest deviation, it has remained one and the same through ten thousand ages. Herein lies its marvel.

The alchemical classics are veiled in secrecy, their teachings couched in metaphors. Misunderstood, yin and yang become sundered; comprehended, water and milk merge as one. The Dao is made clear through texts, and methods are conveyed through spoken instructions. Once the key is revealed, one realizes that the myriad volumes of alchemical scriptures are but repetitions of old words, and cinnabar, mercury, lead, and silver are mere appearances.

Its principle is most subtle; its practice, most simple. Like the dragon painted by Zhang Sengyou, it takes but a touch to the eyes for it to take flight. Without a master’s guidance, it remains a dragon upon the wall, its divine transformations unseen. Thus, those who discourse on the profound and expound on the wondrous in this world differ little from gazing at a lamp through paper or plucking leaves in search of branches—all are but glimpses of the moon through a lattice.

Now, I have committed to these pages what I received from my teacher, comparing it with the *Zhoutong* and *Wuzhen* scriptures, the alchemical formulas left by ancient sages and immortals, and the commentaries of various schools. Selecting the most luminous and essential passages, I have compiled them into a single volume, treasuring it in my chest. The true and supreme Dao is utterly simple and effortless. Through simplicity, the principles of heaven and earth are attained—this is the unbroken thread of truth, not to be lightly transmitted. I record it here for my own delight, not to seek validation from others.

Written on New Year’s Eve of the *guiyou* year of the Jiaqing reign (1813) at the *Weiben* Hall of Chaoyang Mountain.

**By Ji Yi Zi, Fu Jinquan of Jinxī**

---

**General Principles**

1. A hundred thousand words are like drifting upon the vast sea.

The southern pointer’s indication is likewise boundless and without limit. Here, I have taken the essential and pivotal sayings, divided them by category, and made them manifest. Within these pages, the regulation of the cauldron, the gathering of the medicine, the circulation of talismans, the cessation of fire, the opening of barriers, the ingestion of elixirs, embryonic breathing, the projection of spirit, the *Inner Radiance of the Yellow Court*, the *Outer Radiance of the Yellow Court*—none are lacking. May the reader ponder them with care, merging them with the sovereign mind; in time, they will naturally become integrated.

Second, this book broadly gathers from the many scriptures, searching far and wide. Extracting phrases and compiling sayings is like parting the clouds to see the sun, making enlightenment easy to attain. It begins with effort, not clinging to stillness as the sole path of cultivation. Planting virtue comes first, lest one fall into the three peaks in the study of the Dao. Ten thousand volumes of alchemical classics, a legacy of a thousand autumns—every line is a secret model, every word a pearl. Later learners who encounter such passages need not gaze afar at the *Daoist Canon* or exhaustively peruse the books of immortals, for they have already realized the wonder of the *Gate of Non-Duality*.

Third, Bai Ziqing said: “A single word threads through ten thousand volumes of alchemical classics; thus, ten thousand volumes are but one volume, ten thousand words but one word.” He also said: “A word or half a phrase, wondrously penetrating the mystery—what need is there for alchemical books by the thousands?” Alas! How does one come by this word or half-phrase? If you have not yet met a teacher, wait and observe; the verses conceal the method—cultivate well. A torch of wisdom dispels darkness; a compassionate vessel ferries across the sea. Without books, how could this be?

Fourth, the dual cultivation of nature and life originally belongs to one continuous thread. Gathering the medicine and circulating talismans are not two separate principles. Let the discerning take heed.

Fifth, the four essentials—method, resources, companions, and place—none may be lacking. The cauldron and furnace, the talismans and fire, must remain consistent from beginning to end. This must not be overlooked.

Sixth, the work begins with laying the foundation and ends with nurturing the infant. When the medicine ripens and the fire transforms, one sits in effortless dignity. Thereafter, one refines the divine elixir that elevates the dwelling, completes the true emptiness of original nature, and becomes boundless as heaven. Thus begins *Scroll One: General Discourse*.

Wu Jun’s *Treatise on the Mysterious Principle: Chapter on Dao and Virtue* states:

“The Dao is the lineage of emptiness and nothingness, the root of creation and transformation, the origin of spirit and illumination, the source of heaven and earth. Its vastness has no outer limit; its subtlety has no inner boundary. Boundless and without end, darkly obscure and without counterpart. Profoundly hidden and beyond perception, yet its great luminosity sheds light; utterly still and without intent, yet the myriad things take form. Vast and formless, silent and soundless. The ten thousand images arise from it; the five tones are perfected through it. That which is born has its limit; that which is completed must decline. Birth after birth, completion after completion—this has never changed from ancient times to the present. This is called the Dao.

“Virtue is what heaven and earth receive, what yin and yang rely upon. It is ordered by the five phases and woven by the four seasons. Governed by the ruler, instructed by the teacher. The hidden and the manifest, the moving and the still—all find their proper place. Its blessings flow without end, yet living beings do not thank it for its merit; its kindness extends without limit, yet the hundred clans do not rely upon its power. This is called virtue.

“Thus, that which permeates and gives life is called the Dao, and the Dao remains nameless. That which accumulates and brings to fruition is called virtue, and virtue remains unnameable. Let us attempt to discuss this:

“The Supreme Ultimate moves and divides into yin and yang. Yin and yang intermingle and steam forth, giving birth to the ten thousand things. This occurs within heaven and earth, hence the transformations of qi and the patterns of phenomena, the alternation of darkness and light. Yang is luminous and upright; its purest essence is the true record. Yin is dark and perverse; its essence becomes demonic specters. Thus, those born endowed with the numinous yang are wise and sagacious; those nurtured by the dull yin are fierce and stubborn. Yang delights in life, so the wise and sagacious are kind and harmonious. Yin delights in killing, so the fierce and stubborn are harsh and cruel. Those who are sometimes good and sometimes not are born of the balanced union of the two qi—they are the middling people. These three types each receive their endowment—how can teaching be applied to them? Teaching is applied to the middling people. Why? The wise and sagacious know without being taught; the fierce and stubborn cannot be changed even if taught. Both receive the pure qi of yin and yang, just as fire can be extinguished but cannot be made cold, and ice can be melted but cannot be made hot. This is the inherent principle.

“That which gives me life is the Dao; that which endows me is spirit. Yet lifespans differ, and thoughts scatter—departure and abiding do not depend on oneself. Why is this? When nature stirs and becomes emotion, it turns against the Dao. Thus, one is moved by the mechanism of transformation and cannot maintain control. For those who would transcend the realm of existence and cessation, and dwell in the land of self-attainment, the Dao can be their teacher, and spirit their friend.

“What does this mean? The Dao and spirit are without action, yet qi transforms of itself; without thought, yet things are completed of themselves. Among the ranks of beings, one emerges beyond the realm of life and death. Thus, the noble person rejects desires, casts aside cleverness, sees no colors, hears no sounds, abides in tranquility and purity, embodying harmony and clarity of spirit. Forgetting the form in the subtle and the imperceptible, one unites with the utmost essence. This is called returning to one’s origin, reuniting with the Dao. Creation cannot alter it; ghosts and spirits cannot know it—how much less can people? One who is attuned to the Dao may soar beyond the cosmos, yet the mind remains at peace; may rest within the finest space, yet qi circulates of itself. Thus, if the mind is not at peace, one cannot unite with the Dao; if qi is not harmonious, one cannot preserve the form. When the form is preserved and the Dao is united, this is the virtue of heaven and earth. Therefore, one who moves yet does not know they are moving has transcended movement. One who is still yet does not know they are still has gone beyond stillness. One who has transcended movement cannot be pushed by yang. One who has gone beyond stillness cannot be shifted by yin. If yin and yang cannot alter it, how much less can the ten thousand things? Thus, one who is not enticed by things is called *ultimate stillness*; ultimate stillness can unite with *ultimate emptiness*.”

When emptiness reaches its utmost, clarity arises; when clarity reaches its utmost, luminosity emerges; when luminosity reaches its utmost, transparency prevails; when transparency reaches its utmost, the vastness of heaven and earth and the teeming of the ten thousand things cannot escape the mirror of a single square inch.

“The Dao encompasses countless numbers yet is not great; it penetrates the tip of an autumn hair yet is not small. It precedes emptiness and nothingness yet is not the beginning; it follows heaven and earth yet is not the end. It ascends with pure yang yet is not bright; it sinks into heavy yin yet is not dark. It is not inherently numinous, yet when emptiness reaches its utmost, numinosity arises of itself. It is not inherently qi, yet when the numinous moves, qi transforms of itself. Qi has no inherent substance, yet when it congeals and accumulates, form takes shape; form has no inherent nature, yet when it moves and is put to use, nature is diminished. When form takes shape and nature moves, one drifts ever farther from the Dao. Thus, one drowns in life and death, shifts with yin and yang, and cannot come to rest—this is not because the Dao exists and then perishes. If the Dao can generate being from non-being, how much more can it make being merge with non-being? When being merges with non-being, being does not perish.

“Yin and yang unite, and humans are born. The hun is the yang spirit; the po is the yin soul. They coalesce as an embryo, circulate qi, model the body, and distinguish form. Yet if their power is not complete, each returns to its origin. Thus, when yin prevails, yang is exhausted and one dies; when yang prevails, yin dissolves and one becomes an immortal. Gentleness, kindness, integrity, and purity are yang. Harshness, jealousy, lewdness, and turbidity are yin. When the mind is tranquil and empty, yang harmony pervades. When intention is restless and desirous, yin qi enters. Understanding these two, when yang prevails and yin is subdued, one begins the path to long life.

“The Dao does not desire a mind, for if there is a mind, true qi does not gather. Yet it does not desire the absence of mind, for if there is no mind, external perversities take residence. Thus, when my mind does not incline, nothing is not upright. When thoughts attach to things, nothing is not perverse. The coming of perversity or uprightness lies with me alone. Therefore, those of superior learning who are negligent in preserving mindfulness are overcome by the yin corpse. Those who delight in diligent purity are overcome by the yang spirit. Those who alternate between negligence and diligence remain in unresolved struggle. The next level lies in disciplining conduct and fortifying integrity, making essence single-minded and undistracted. Those who persevere in this will see the corpse dissolve and the spirit preside—this is called yang prevailing. When yang prevails, one draws near the Dao.

“When the body moves yet the mind is still, when the spirit is concentrated yet the traces shift—this is non-action. When one dwells in leisure yet the spirit is agitated, when one sits in silence yet the mind wanders—this is action. Non-action brings order; action brings chaos. Though non-action is supremely easy, only those of utmost clarity can fathom it. Heaven and earth engage in non-action by day and by night; in non-action they are one, yet order and chaos differ. Why? By day, non-action is not brightness, so the host of yang appears and the multitude of yin hides. By night, non-action is not darkness, so the multitude of yin moves and the host of yang rests. Thus, when the ruler is enlightened and practices non-action, loyal ministers advance, treacherous officials withdraw, and the world is ordered. When the ruler is benighted and engages in action, loyal ministers retreat, treacherous officials take office, and the world falls into chaos. Therefore, the wise practice non-action with wisdom, while the deluded practice non-action in confusion. Wisdom leads to union with the Dao; confusion leads to identification with things.

“Those of superior learning at times lodge their aspirations in the lofty and distant, dwelling contentedly in the realm of self-attainment, harmonizing with true immortals they might meet, ascending to the celestial rivers—they are governed by the spirit. Though they are governed by the spirit, they must still guard against the dissipation of yang harmony and the encroachment of yin perversities. They may enter a quiet chamber, pacify the mind, suppress arising thoughts, and abide in stillness and single-mindedness, so that the spirit does not scatter and yang numinosity remains whole. They must be careful not to indulge in feelings of ease and joy, lest yin perversities draw near. If at times they grow restless, competitive, or agitated, this is because the various po within the body are being refined by yang numinous qi, and the accumulated stagnation of the yin corpse is about to disperse and disturb the true within the Crimson Palace. They may enter a quiet chamber, maintain a firm grasp, await the spirit’s clarity and the mind’s calm, and unite with emptiness and stillness—this too is a method for cleansing the mind.

“Let speech not exceed conduct; let conduct not shame the mind. When conduct continues without cease, heaven and earth cherish it, and the divine luminaries protect it. Calamity and violence have no way to approach; ghosts and spirits cannot disturb. But if one’s speech is passionate yet conduct is turbid, if one’s reputation is pure yet one’s traces are defiled, though shame may be concealed externally, the mind feels remorse within. Heaven and earth detest it; the divine luminaries strike it down. Though one may strive mightily for the Dao, it cannot be attained. Thus, it is better to endure others’ slander than to incur heaven’s censure. Human slander may yet be dispelled, but heaven’s punishment cannot be evaded.

“What the Dao most abhors is lewdness, killing, and yin treachery—these are truly easy to avoid. As for minor likes and dislikes, right and wrong, approval and disapproval, the common emotions of humanity, these are exceedingly difficult to discern. Even the slightest matter, if it concerns the square inch of the mind, when thoughts are still and the spirit at ease, perverse qi cannot enter. When my will is undisturbed, the true person becomes my companion. Those who seek praise and resent slander are not exempt even among the worthy and enlightened. Yet if one examines oneself and finds no goodness yet receives praise, this is inauspicious. If one reflects on oneself and finds no fault yet is slandered, what harm is there?

“The essence of yang is the hun and the spirit; the essence of yin is the corpse and the po. When the spirit prevails, one does good; when the corpse is strong, one does evil. Restraining evil and promoting good brings order; forgetting good and indulging evil brings chaos. When order endures, the corpse perishes and the po is refined. When chaos endures, the spirit departs and the hun dissolves. When the corpse perishes and the po is refined, the spirit and form unite to become an immortal. When the spirit departs and the hun dissolves, the corpse and po merge to become a ghost. This is the natural Dao.

“The eyes delight in bright colors as their splendor; the mind values fame and reputation as its treasure. The body favors light and fine adornments; the mouth craves rare and exquisite flavors. The ears covet beautiful sounds; the nose delights in fragrant scents. These six all corrupt virtue, harm nature, and fell the numinous radiance.”

Thus, what one possesses should be kept at a distance, while what one lacks need not be sought. Only clothing and food—so vital to people—are also the greatest hindrances for those who pursue the Dao. Yet if one surrenders the heart to fate and lets things take their course, nothing will fail to provide what is needed. Even as Heaven and Earth bring forth birds and beasts, they cover them with feathers and supply them with insects and grains—how much more so for humans? The key lies in forgetting one’s aims and following nature’s course. When awake, one merges with yang; when asleep, one unites with yin. Too much waking strengthens the *hun*; too much sleep fortifies the *po*. Those with strong *hun* belong to the living; those with strong *po* belong to the dead. If one consumes primordial harmony and refines away flavors, keeping the spirit clear and qi refreshed, even to the point of remaining awake day and night—nothing surpasses this in goodness. Yet if the human heart indulges this for long, it becomes vast and forgets to return. If one abruptly halts it, the heart leaps beyond all bounds. Indulgence obscures one’s nature; restraint wearies the primordial spirit. How then can the seeker of the Dao attain stillness?

For nature is fundamentally most serene, yet stirred by external things. Once habituated to movement, how can it suddenly find peace? Having grown accustomed to movement and been carried away by it, one may instead cultivate stillness and attain tranquility. Thus, those who cultivate well find abundance in quietude; those who cultivate poorly grow troubled without result. Therefore, when agitation arises, restrain it with calm; when deviance arises, guard it with correctness; when seeking arises, suppress it with renunciation; when turbidity arises, clarify it with purity. Leisurely and carefree, without desire or scheming—acting thus, halting thus, even in haste, one remains thus, wandering freely. With long practice, external things grow dim while the inner spirit becomes clear. One does not think of stillness yet attains it naturally.

The ancients said, “Accumulated habits become nature.” Is this not what they meant?

Someone asked: “Many have sought immortality through the ages, yet few have attained the Dao. Why is this?”

The answer: “Of a thousand who study the Dao, only one truly understands it. Of a thousand who understand it, only one is devoted to it. Of a thousand devoted to it, only one is single-minded. Of a thousand single-minded, only one persists long enough. Thus, many study, but few succeed.”

Another asked: “Immortality is humanity’s highest beauty, and death its greatest horror—everyone knows this. Yet why do so many follow the common path, while so few cultivate immortality?”

The answer: “There is one reason: if one’s endowment of qi is not lofty, one’s aspirations cannot be vast. Thus, one drowns in immediate concerns, forgets far-reaching vision, is ensnared by sensual pleasures, and dulled by desires. The Dao of immortality values substance; the human path values splendor. The Dao of immortality and human nature are directly opposed. All evils can be abandoned; all virtues can be cultivated. With perfect conduct, a pure body, and lifelong effort—without slackening or despair—one may embrace the Dao even in death without compromising integrity.

Generally, one must cultivate blessings externally and refine the spirit internally. With deep merit in both, the steps to immortality may be ascended, and the grotto-heavens explored. Those who have attained the Dao through the ages all relied on the mutual support of blessings and wisdom. Wisdom is the lamp’s flame; blessings are the lamp’s oil. Without oil, the flame does not shine; without blessings, wisdom does not endure. Thus, the enlightened would sooner harm their bodies than diminish their blessings. Though one may be naturally brilliant and learned, without accumulated blessings, one cannot fathom the Dao’s profound mysteries.

The sages and immortals of past and present share the same Dao, though their blessings differ. Those with greater external merit ascend to higher immortal ranks; those with lesser merit occupy lower stations. Thus, celestial sages whose virtuous deeds were insufficient may be sent back to the human realm to repay past debts. The human world is turbid and difficult to cultivate, yet merit accrues swiftly; the celestial realms are pure and easy to inhabit, yet merit accumulates slowly. The Yellow Emperor dwelled long in the heavens but, for debating grand deeds, was cast down to the mortal world. In his first life, he was a commoner; in his second, a minister; in his third, a sovereign. Only after aiding beings and benefiting life did he complete his merit and ascend as an immortal.

Even the underworld resembles the human realm, with every inch of land and span of sky under governance. All who rule there were once people of merit in the world.

My original heart is empty, like the face it had before birth—what is it, truly? Vast and obscure, fleeting and indistinct, it cannot be known by knowing, nor grasped by grasping. Forced to name it, we call it the Dao, the spirit, the nature, the destiny—yet it is only the heart. Seen thus, is it not vast? Is it not noble? Yet why, though bound to the cycle of birth and death, can one not master oneself? Because a single thought stirs within, and the six senses wander without. If one does not turn toward goodness, one drifts toward evil. Thus, there are the karmic retributions of heaven and hell, the six paths of rebirth without end. Is this not agonizing? Is this not lamentable?

The enlightened, however, are not thus. Through fasting and abstinence, they illumine their virtue. With a single truth settled and clear, all misfortunes vanish. From then on, they guard their speech, moderate their diet, eliminate impurities, halt stray thoughts, purify the heart, and hold to unity. Emptiness, tranquility, stillness, non-action—withdrawing sight and returning hearing, harmonizing with the world, they see sudden thoughts as illness and non-continuation as medicine. They do not fear thoughts arising, only fear awakening too late. Just as one daily tends to one’s hair, why not tend to body and mind thus? A single thought arising is delusion; that which transcends past and present without decay is the eternal. Is not the true and constant the mark of the great person?”

Master Shangyang said: “The Dao—it is the position of Heaven and Earth, the nurturing of the myriad things; it is the raising of the sun and moon, the generation of the Five Phases; it is more numerous than the sands of the Ganges; solitary, it has no peer; it enters the primordial chaos and returns to the primordial depths; it gathers the workings of creation and instantly transcends the sacred and the mundane; in the unmanifested moment before signs arise, it suddenly attains numinous communion; in the clear distinction of life and death before the eyes, none can escape it; dwelling in lowliness and filth, it is supremely noble; abiding in darkness and obscurity, it is utterly luminous.”

This Dao—it possesses the eye of great insight yet lacks pupils, the ear of great wisdom yet hears nothing, the mouth that drinks the Western River yet has no teeth, the nose of all wondrous fragrances yet does not smell, the tongue that slays and revives yet knows no taste, the vajra dharmakāya yet abides in freedom, the sword of life and death yet no warrior dares wield it, the single-character meaning yet no scholar can describe it. Though shrouded in boundless darkness, it suffers no diminution; though obscure, it is called luminous. Steep and towering, standing sheer as a ten-thousand-fathom cliff, it casts forth the body without fear. Subtle enough to enter a mote of dust, vast enough to embrace heaven and earth, it transforms nonbeing into being, becoming Buddha or immortal. The fifty-four hundred and eight volumes of Buddhist scriptures cannot exhaust its reaches; the thirty-three chapters of the *Doctrine of the Mean* cannot fathom its depths; the five thousand words of the *Dao De Jing* cannot plumb its ultimate extent.

What, then, is this Dao? In a single word, define it: qi.

This qi moves—nothing in heaven and earth does not arise from it. Beyond heaven and earth, it enfolds them; within heaven and earth, it circulates through them. Sun, moon, stars, and constellations derive their brightness from it; wind, clouds, thunder, and rain derive their movement from it; the four seasons and all things derive their birth, growth, harvest, and storage from it. This is none other than the qi of yin and yang, the creative transformation of heaven and earth.

Yet within the human body alone is the complete qi of heaven and earth’s yin and yang, creative transformation. Obtain and employ it, match it with one’s true mercury, and immediately perfect the supreme Dao. The *Yellow Court Classic* says: “Feeding alone on the great harmony of yin and yang’s qi, thus one does not die and heaven’s appearance is complete.” It also says: “Immortal and Daoist are not divine; they accumulate essence and amass qi to become real.” And again: “Emerging in clarity and entering in mystery, the two qi intermingle; if you encounter them, you ascend to the Milky Way.”

The *Book of Changes* says: “The sun departs, the moon arrives; the moon departs, the sun arrives.” It also says: “Opening and closing are called change; going and coming without end are called penetration.” Zhang Hengqu said: “That humans have breath is the image of hardness and softness rubbing together, of Qian and Kun opening and closing.” The *Purple Yang’s Admonition on Regulating the Breath* states: “The unfolding and gathering of yinyun—its wonders are boundless. Who presides over it? The achievement of the non-governing.”

*The Master Who Embraces Simplicity* states: “I have examined and perused books on nurturing life and gathered methods for prolonging vision, the scrolls and volumes I have traversed numbering in the thousands. All regard the Reverted Elixir and Golden Liquid as the great essentials—these two matters being the ultimate of the immortal Dao.

In former times, the central states fell into chaos and upheaval, and none failed to scatter and flee in all directions. I traveled among the several provinces of Xu, Yu, Jing, Xiang, Jiang, and Guang, encountering many wandering Daoist practitioners. Some were so renowned that their names seemed to dwell beyond the clouds and sun. Yet in the end, they were all alike: what they knew and saw, whether shallow or deep, present or absent, was not enough to surpass one another. Whenever I inquired about the Divine Elixir, the Golden Liquid, the *Writings of the Three Sovereigns*, or the methods for summoning the spirits of Heaven and Earth, not a single one knew of them.

Formerly, Zuo Yuanfang concentrated his thoughts on Mount Tianzhu, where divine beings bestowed upon him the *Canon of the Golden Elixir and Immortality*. At the great upheaval of the Han’s end, he had no leisure to compound it and instead fled to the lands east of the Jiang. His aspiration was to seek out a famous mountain to cultivate this Way. My great-uncle, the Immortal Master, received it from Yuanfang. In all, he was given the *Three Scrolls of the Great Clarity Elixir Canon*, the *One Scroll of the Nine-Cauldron Elixir Canon*, and the *One Scroll of the Golden Liquid Elixir Canon*.

My teacher, Master Zheng, was a disciple of my great-uncle the Immortal Master. He, too, received these from my great-uncle, but being poor and unable to afford the ingredients, I personally served him, sweeping and cleaning for many years. At last, in the mountains of Maji, he established an altar, swore an oath, and transmitted them to me, along with all the oral instructions not recorded in writing. These books were previously unknown east of the Jiang; they originated with Zuo Yuanfang, who passed them to my great-uncle, who passed them to Master Zheng, who passed them to me. Thus, no other Daoist practitioners knew of them.

One who drinks jade nectar knows the thinness of mere water and duckweed; one who gazes upon Kunlun knows the lowliness of mere mounds and anthills. To behold the Way of the Golden Elixir makes one no longer wish to look upon lesser medical texts. Yet the great medicine is ultimately difficult to obtain, and one must for now rely on minor medicines to sustain oneself. Though one were to consume ten thousand measures of other medicines, they could never grant long life.

In the world, there may be those who love the Dao yet never encounter this method, never meet a true teacher, and thus never hear that such wondrous matters exist in the world. I have now briefly excerpted the essentials of the Golden Elixir to compare and present them to those of like aspiration who come after.”

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* states:
"Men say that life, to me, is of the greatest benefit. In weighing its worth, though one were enthroned as emperor, it could not compare to this method. In measuring its weight, though one possessed all under heaven, it could not be exchanged for this art. Thus arises the parable of the dead king who envies the living mouse."

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* states:
"All men eagerly pursue power, profit, and sensual desires. Yet if my own body ceases to exist, though I held high office and boundless authority, though gold and jade piled like mountains and beauties numbered in the thousands—none of it would be mine."

*The Master Embracing Simplicity* states:
"According to the immortals’ scriptures, all who attain transcendence do so because their fate aligns with the qi of divine immortality, an endowment they receive naturally. Thus, even within the womb, they already bear the nature of the Dao. When awareness dawns, their hearts incline toward such matters, and they must encounter an enlightened teacher to obtain the method. Otherwise, though told of it, they do not believe; though believing, they do not seek. From ancient times to the present, there have been those of towering talent and keen insight who disbelieved in immortals, and those of modest ability who studied and attained transcendence. One may be broadly discerning yet obstructed in matters of immortality; another may grasp little yet suddenly comprehend its principles. Is this not the work of fate?"

The *Baopuzi* states: "Time flies like an arrow, swift and fleeting—scarcely has youth passed before old age arrives. Nothing compares to its haste. A lifespan of a hundred years amounts to little more than thirty thousand days. In infancy and weakness, one knows nothing; in decline and senility, joy and pleasure fade away. Between childhood and dotage, a few decades remain, yet peril, hardship, sorrow, and illness follow one after another. Of the years spent in this world, roughly half are consumed. Even for those who live a full century, days of peace, laughter, and harmony scarcely exceed fifty or sixty years. In a mere breath, all is extinguished—grief, sorrow, and the haze of old age claim no more than six or seven thousand days, and in the blink of an eye, they too are gone. How many, then, truly reach a hundred years? Fewer than one in ten thousand. Reflecting deeply, one finds no cause to mock the mayfly or the morning mushroom. As the saying goes, 'A man in this world loses a day with each passing day, like an ox or sheep led to the slaughterhouse—with every step forward, death draws nearer.' The wise do not grieve over death, not because they do not seek to avoid it, but because they know no means to escape it. Worrying in vain serves no purpose, so they say, 'Rejoice in Heaven, follow fate, and do not fret.' How could they truly wish not to live forever?"

The *Baopuzi* states: "Of all round-headed, breath-bearing beings, who does not cherish life and fear death? Yet glory, splendor, power, and profit tempt the mind; unblemished complexions and jade-like skin dazzle the eyes; clear melodies and flowing tones confuse the ears; love, desire, gain, and harm disturb the spirit; fame, reputation, and renown bind the body. These things come unbidden and take shape without effort. Unless one perceives the truth alone, discerns change beyond the ordinary, wields clear judgment in the realm of the profound, awakens to the closeness and distance of fame and reputation, and laments the lightning speed of passing time—how can one cast aside fleeting pleasures, restrain desires, sever immediate cravings, and pursue the distant work of immortality? All things arise from non-being; form depends on spirit to exist. Being is the palace of non-being; form is the dwelling of spirit. Compare it to a dike: if the dike breaks, the water does not remain. Compare it to a candle: if the candle melts away, the flame does not abide. When the body labors, the spirit scatters; when qi is exhausted, life ends. If the root is depleted while the branches flourish, the verdant leaves will fall from the tree. If qi is wearied and desire prevails, the essence and spirit will depart the body. The departed never return; once decayed, there is no principle of rebirth. How pitiful are those who comprehend the Dao! Is it not for good reason they value the shade over jade? Thus, they esteem lofty heights as mere excrescences and weigh all things as lightly as a cicada’s wing—not out of idle boasting or forced disdain for worldly affairs, but because their vision is clear, and they abandon such things as if forgetting them entirely."

The *Baopuzi* states: "When the elixir is completed, the entire household ascends as immortals—not merely one person."

The *Baopuzi* states: "Those who know this Way—what need have they for kings and lords? Once the divine elixir is perfected, not only does one attain longevity, but one can also transmute base metals into gold."

The *Baopuzi* states: "Once this Way is accomplished, one may achieve immortality. The Way of longevity is the supreme Way, which is why the ancients held it in such reverence."

The *Baopuzi* states: "Who can fully comprehend these profound and esoteric methods? Yet once one grasps their essentials, they need not await the arrival of sages or great talents to practice them—even ordinary people can achieve this."

The *Baopuzi* states: "Though one may live forever without dying, the original body remains unchanged. If one possesses this Way, what difficulty could there be?"

The *Baopuzi* states: "My master once told his disciples: 'When a person seeks the Dao as if distressed by poverty or troubled by lowly status, how could they fail to attain it? The obstacle lies only in a lack of steadfast resolve, in pursuing the near while forgetting the distant, in delighting upon hearing the teachings and leaning forward eagerly—only to soon forget them entirely. A tiny sapling may grow incrementally, but the erosion of a mountain is ceaseless; how then can one fathom the subtleties of the ultimate teachings or achieve the boundless heights of the Way?'

Zhang Ziyang’s *Preface to the Wuzhen Pian* says: 'Alas! The human body is hard to obtain, yet time slips away swiftly. How can one measure the span of life or escape karmic retribution? If one does not awaken in time but merely resigns to fate and awaits death, a single misguided thought at the final moment may cast one into the three evil destinies, where aeons may pass without hope of liberation. At such a time, what use is regret? Thus, the Yellow Emperor and Laozi opened the gate of expedient means through the study of *xingming* (nature and destiny), teaching people to cultivate the seed of immortality to escape the cycle of birth and death.'"

In the *San Zhu Wuzhen*, Daoguang Zu says: "Even if one’s discourse flows like a cascading river, it amounts to no more than idle talk of Chan or discussion of the Dao, or solitary sitting in vain cultivation. Though one may pass through countless kalpas, one will never nourish life to attain longevity. This Way is not something one may hear of in the mundane world; only those of great virtue and goodness are permitted to seek it."

Shangyangzi of the *San Zhu* says: "Alas! Every step is a difficult endeavor—how dare one act recklessly? How dare one speak carelessly? There are those in the world who are seeds of hell, who open their mouths only to speak of Chan or discuss the Dao, deceiving others until their dying day without remorse. Then there are those of petty cleverness who, without studying the practices of immortals and sages, slander the path of cultivation, claiming that since all that is born must die, how could there be a Way of enduring vision? These are the seeds of hell, content to revolve endlessly in the cycle of rebirth."

The *San Zhu* records Lu Ziye as saying: "The Way of the inner elixir closely resembles the work of external alchemy with furnace and fire. Generally speaking, mercury cannot be subdued without lead. Those who understand external practices will find the internal ones easy to grasp.

"It begins with effortful action and ends in effortless non-doing. The realm of non-doing is where true immortals dwell." (Ji Yizi remarks: "Those who sit idly in solitary practice, vainly hoping for accomplishment, are like placing the feet where the head should be—truly a case of proceeding backward.")

The *San Zhu* further records Daoguang Zu as saying: "Fools claim that my teaching is like the Chan school, where a single word brings sudden enlightenment and Buddhahood. This is nothing but deception to delude the ignorant—how could such a thing be possible? Know this: the golden elixir is the most sublime and wondrous vehicle in my teaching."

Shangyangzi says: "Those who cultivate this Way must dwell in the Five Defiled Evil Realms while practicing the Dharma that transcends the world." He also states: "To refine the elixir, one must first accumulate hidden virtue."

Chaoyuanzi says: "Life and death are said to be determined by Heaven and Earth, yet one’s nature and destiny originally belong to mercury and lead. Does this not mean that my destiny lies in my own hands, not in Heaven’s?"

The *Huangfu Long’s Memorial in Response to Emperor Wu of Wei* states: "I have heard that among all things between Heaven and Earth, none is more noble than man. Of all that is noble in man, nothing is more precious than life. From the primordial chaos of distant antiquity to the endless cycles of kalpas, man’s existence between them passes as swiftly as lightning. Each time I reflect on this, my heart grows heavy with sorrow. Life does not come twice, and what has passed cannot be reclaimed. Why not then master the passions, nurture one’s nature, and preserve oneself, attaining boundless longevity? This can only be achieved through cultivating the Dao. The Dao is easily known—yet none can practice it."

Qiu Changchun says: "The Dao encompasses Heaven and Earth, and the spirit governs the hundred forms. That which is born and dies is form; that which is neither born nor dies is spirit and nature. All that has form must perish—even Heaven and Earth are but illusory bodies. When the kalpas end, they too shall cease, yet a single ray of yang light transcends the bounds of kalpic destiny. Within the human body, it is the ocean of nature, the primordial spirit."

Shi Xinglin says: "All things that live must die, but the primordial spirit, though it dies, lives again. When the spirit returns within the qi, the elixir path naturally comes to fruition."

Shangyangzi’s *Commentary on the Cantong Qi* states: "People, relying on their cleverness and clinging to their views without yielding, claim that if there is birth, there must be death—how could there be immortality? They say that sages, immortals, and buddhas are all born of Heaven—how could a master confer them? How could man achieve them? Failing to reflect deeply, they resign themselves to death. They ought to consider: from whence does this body arise? If it is born of the yin and yang qi of father and mother, then surely that same yin and yang qi can prolong life and lead to the attainment of sagehood, immortality, or buddhahood.

"The merit of the sages is shared with later generations. Yet I fear that students lack the great good fortune, the great wisdom, to bear such a transmission. Bound by a thousand obstructions, they cannot awaken to the truth."

White Ziqing’s *Preface to the "Pointing to the Mystery"* states: "Some claim I abandon my wife and family to cultivate in solitude; others say I retreat into the deep mountains in search of stillness; still others insist I abstain from meat and wine to maintain purity. Such divergent paths only lead one further from the Dao.

"Later seekers of immortality’s Way—why must you flee to the mountains, shun the world, forsake children and spouse, renounce meat and wine, or fast in purity? These are all misguided efforts, far removed from the Dao."

Hanshanzi said: "Those who cultivate the Dao must cast off cravings and desires, conserve their spirit and embrace harmony—thus they remain unburdened. They restrain the mind within and harmonize the body without—thus they avoid transgressions. They put others before themselves, embrace softness, and guard humility—thus they secure their well-being. They extend goodness to others while attributing faults to themselves—thus they accumulate virtue. The merit need not be great, so long as it is established without slack; the error need not be grave, so long as it is abandoned without repetition—thus they accumulate merit. Only then, when inner conduct is full and the outer elixir arrives, may one hope to attain the Dao. As for those who have not uprooted the Three Poisons yet adorn themselves with fine robes and caps—are they not but saddles on tigers and leopards, mere hides of oxen and sheep? What is there to value in them?"

The Perfected One Xie Ziran said: "If one can maintain purity, burn incense in a quiet room, and recite the *Huangting* or *Daode* scriptures—whether once or seven times—it far surpasses acts of almsgiving. The key lies in the sincerity of the heart, not the number of recitations. Those who lack devotion and falter midway incur even greater harm—beware, beware! In paying homage to revered images, four bows are weighty, while three are slight. Those who deeply contemplate and expound the Dao gain blessings, while the careless invite misfortune."

*The Discourse of the Sea Voyager* states: "The Dao takes rarity and stillness as its root, profound tranquility as its foundation. Sever desires to dwell in the mountains, relinquish greed to live in the world. Preserve the spirit and nurture qi, consume cypress and pine—only then may one gradually attain cool clarity and glimpse the true realm. Gold and silver are but worldly things, briefly aiding fleeting life. If it is your portion, they come unbidden; if not, no amount of seeking will obtain them. To disperse them is to manifest virtue; to hoard them is to invite calamity upon the body. You common folk know nothing of the supreme Dao. Insatiable in greed and attachment, you delight only in amassing jade and gold; endlessly indulgent, you pride yourselves on fine furs and fat horses. Not a day passes without craving rich flavors, not an hour without clinging to splendor. The seven apertures flow unceasingly, the Three Fields remain unsecured. Even if you consume myriad potent medicines, what benefit is there? How can you transcend the world? Follow my words, and the great Dao is not distant—the true realm will surely be reached."

Shangyangzi said: "To reveal the mechanisms of Heaven and Earth’s creation, to exhaust the virtue of Qian and Kun’s nurturance, to illuminate the principle of the sun and moon’s conjoined brightness, to disclose the workings of yin and yang’s inverted operation—*The Book of Changes* states: 'Align your virtue with Heaven and Earth, your brightness with the sun and moon, your order with the four seasons, your auspices with ghosts and spirits. Precede Heaven, and it will not oppose you; follow Heaven, and you will accord with its timing. If even Heaven does not oppose you, how much less can man?' Thus, those who master this Dao are sages; those who practice it are divine beings."

Jiyizi sighed: "Only divine beings can preserve such numinous power without flaw. Alas! How subtle it is!"

Sun Zhenren said: "For attaining long life and ascending as an immortal, only the Golden Elixir suffices; for preserving form and reversing aging, only the True Unity excels—thus do immortals esteem it. Though the methods of contemplation and visualization number in the thousands, they are ultimately burdensome and distracting. Yet if one masters the Way of Guarding the One, all else becomes effortless."

*The True Words of Chanting the Dao* states: "The sages of antiquity, in composing their books and establishing their teachings, never once wrote the character for 'elixir.' The term *dan* arose only in the Han dynasty. The Great Elixir is formless, soundless, supremely numinous, and wondrously subtle."

*The Transformed Writings of Wenchang* records: "The Most High said: 'The *Great* aligns with the Dao and merges with the True; the *Elixir* unites with the heart as one. After you later attain the five penetrations in full, you will be beyond comparison with your former self.' Between Heaven and Earth, there is only this one yin and one yang. In its fundamental essence, it is called the Dao; in its transformative mechanism, it is called the *Yi*; in its divine function, it is called the Elixir.

The yin and yang of the *Yi*’s Way do not extend beyond Qian and Kun; the yin and yang of the Elixir’s Way do not transcend nature and life—Qian and Kun *are* nature and life. Yet one must thoroughly investigate the subtle movements prior to their arising to understand how Heaven and Earth originate from this, how the human body begins from this, and how the foundation of the Elixir’s Way is established here."

Zhu Yuanyu said: "To seek the Supreme Dao, one must cultivate virtue to align with Heaven. When the numinous elixir enters the abdomen, life is determined by me, not by Heaven. This is true—but it applies only to those who have already attained mastery. As for the initial steps, there are both ease and difficulty, which cannot be generalized. Generally, bearing the task with a sincere heart makes it easy; seizing it with a cunning or false heart makes it difficult. Only with a truly sincere heart can one ascend to Heaven. If even the slightest cunning or falsity intrudes, it immediately severs the connection with Heaven’s mind, inviting demonic obstructions.

Thus, though the work of shaping destiny depends on me, the opportunity for transcending the world truly depends on Heaven. The accumulation of hidden virtue cannot be neglected. Since ancient times, all sages and perfected beings have universally aided others with expedient means and extended compassion to all things. *The Scripture of Heavenly Response* should be practiced at every moment. In such practice, one must settle the heart and willingly engage, acting without ulterior motive. One must not harbor even the slightest thought of calculating merit, scheming for gain, seeking blessings, or expecting reward. Should a single impure念 arise, it will violate the heart and offend Heaven, casting one into the retinue of demons and obstructing the causal conditions for the Great Dao.

All demonic obstructions in the world arise from the mind. With a heart that accumulates virtue, one naturally aligns with Heaven. The mechanism of cultivating the Dao thus depends on me, not on Heaven. Therefore, the study of shaping destiny pertains not only to the stage of mastery but equally to the initial steps. Those who resolve to shoulder the Great Dao should reflect on this thrice."

Ziye said: "The Great Dao is transmitted but once in ten thousand kalpas—it is no trivial matter. The higher the Dao, the more formidable the demons. Without the support of hidden virtue, one risks the misfortune of wavering resolve."

The Lazy Daoist said: "The immortal’s path to long life, if not accompanied by hidden virtue, lacks the foundation to receive Heaven’s blessings and fortune. Even if one hears of the Great Dao, cultivation will be difficult to complete. Yet practicing hidden virtue is the silent cultivation of the heart-ground. Only when all roots of fame are severed and one transcends life to become an immortal will ghosts and spirits submit.

When others inflict harm upon me, I perceive only that I have awakened the karmic obstacles of beings. To blame myself and pity others—this alone holds meaning as the great compassion. As the saying goes, 'Reflecting inwardly, I find propriety; I do not contend with them'—if one acts thus, it is no different from the Heaven and Earth that cover and bear all. What virtue is there in that? Alas! Such a one is far from the immortal’s path."

The *Singing the Dao’s True Words* states: "Those who study the Dao take saving the world and delivering others as their fundamental duty, not merely seeking personal immortality and deathlessness. Clearly, they must act for the sake of the beings in the Three Wretched Realms and Eight Difficulties, the Six Paths and Four Births—this multitude of sentient beings, all parents to one another. Seeing their suffering, one must taste it as if it were one’s own; seeing their delusion, one must feel as if drowning in it oneself. Only with such compassion and such earnest vows will one’s cultivation naturally draw the manifestation of all sages and the attendance of all spirits, who will fear only that one’s Dao may not be accomplished.

The immortals’ search for worthy disciples surpasses even the disciples’ search for immortals. The sages and worthies of the upper realms cherish those disciples who endure hardship as if they were priceless pearls or jade. Pearls have a price, but a good disciple is beyond value. From the very beginning, when one first sets one’s resolve, one’s name and native place are recorded in the celestial registers, and daily the sages and worthies descend to examine one’s merits and faults. If one’s resolve is true and one’s devotion steadfast, the sages rejoice beyond measure, protecting one from the empty void as tenderly as a mother cradles her infant.

All my disciples must rouse great courage and make great vows, striving to accomplish the work of one who stands firm between heaven and earth, the hero who turns the cosmos and revolves the heavens. They must greatly invigorate the mystic wind and widely open the halls of the Dharma."

Master Ma Danyang said:
"Swiftly nurture the elixir-pearl, swiftly nurture the body;
With hidden virtue well bestow, deliver this people.
If this body is not delivered in this life,
In what life shall this body be delivered?"

Master Shangyang said:
"This Dao is most vast, stealing heaven and earth’s secrets,
pilfering the workings of creation.
Only after accumulating virtue and cultivating blessings
can one bear its weight.
As the *Awakening to Reality* says:
'The great medicine’s cultivation may be easy or hard—
know that it depends on me, yet also on heaven.
Without accumulating deeds and nurturing hidden virtue,
demons in throngs will arise as obstacles.'

What is hidden virtue?
To give without seeking repayment—this is hidden virtue.
To accumulate goodness unknown to others—this is hidden virtue.
Not to drive others into peril—this is hidden virtue.
To aid in secret—this is hidden virtue.

For the cultivator,
if hidden virtue is not full,
rarely will one not be assailed by external demons.
But if one can turn inward in reflection,
rouse great forbearance and diligent progress,
then demonic obstructions transform into hidden virtue.
As the *Sutra* says:
'When misfortune comes, I return with blessing.
When resentment comes, I return with virtue.'
All this is the power of abundant hidden virtue,
the merit of dispelling demons.

'Deeds must reach eight hundred, merit must fill three thousand'—
it seems as though lifetimes would not suffice.
Yet know that eight hundred and three thousand
are all created by the mind alone.
If one can turn the mind in a single thought,
lay down the whole body,
and make the square inch within empty and vast,
then all is instantly resolved.
The three thousand merits, the eight hundred deeds,
are fulfilled in the present moment,
and one’s breadth and luminosity
become one with the great void of heaven."

The *True Words on Singing the Dao* says:
"If one can wield heroic talent for the learning of sages and worthies,
cultivate the practice of vigilance in solitude to nurture the vast qi,
then in days to come, ascending to heaven,
one will surely occupy a lofty position,
deliver the suffering in darkness,
bless descendants with fortune,
and in achievements and renown—
is this not grand?

Yet I see vulgar scholars
who possess not a foot of goodness nor an inch of excellence
to surpass the common crowd,
yet vainly aspire to the arts of Kan and Li, water and fire.
With worldly attachments still unremoved,
how can the immortal embryo form?
Their aim is mere warmth and fullness,
while they dream of purity and emptiness.
Does this not make the countless lofty perfected ones
of the Great Canopy Heaven burst into laughter?

In the beginning, before the primal chaos,
when the one qi had not yet taken form,
it could not be spoken of, nor named.
Vast and empty, boundless and formless—
this is called true emptiness.
Within emptiness, not empty—
this is called wondrous being.
Only by being empty within being
is the beginningless beginning
forcibly named the beginning of heaven and earth.
Only by being being within emptiness
is the beginning of being
forcibly named the mother of the myriad things.

Being within emptiness—
this is the Supreme Ultimate’s root in the Ultimateless.
Emptiness within being—
this is the Ultimateless becoming the Supreme Ultimate.

Before father and mother were born,
perfection was all-pervading,
vast and penetrating, spiritually luminous,
inherently unstained,
requiring no cultivation or verification.
Within emptiness, not empty—
this is the true sovereign of the void,
what is called the one Supreme Ultimate throughout the whole.
Then a single spark of numinous light
comes from the great void,
suddenly sensing and attaching,
entering straight into the central palace of the spirit-chamber,
becoming the master of this body—
what is called each possessing a Supreme Ultimate."

Zhu Yuanyu said:
"Between heaven and earth,
mountains, rivers, earth, and stones are all obstructed and impassable.
Only the grotto-heavens and empty valleys
are interconnected, opening into one another.
The human body is likewise:
flesh, muscles, and bones are all obstructed and impassable.
Only the mysterious aperture and empty valley
are interconnected, pulse upon pulse,
resembling the grotto-heavens of creation.
The primordial qi comes and goes,
vast and boundless,
precisely within the empty valley.

Having known that emptiness does not obstruct being,
having known that being does not obstruct emptiness—
at this stage,
root, dust, consciousness, and thought all dissolve.
The great earth and mountains and rivers
are all like illusory reflections.
This body is not even my own—
what then of home?
What then of fields and gardens, wife and children?
All these things outside the body.

Worldly commoners
suffer and cling to fields and gardens,
attach to wife and children,
and while a single breath remains,
refuse to let go.
Little do they know that what the commoner most covets
is precisely where the Daoist finds great liberation."

Master Shangyang’s *Seven Admonitions for Vigilance*:
As the *Awakening to Reality* says:
"Follow the time to gather, determining sinking and floating;
advance the fire, but guard against peril."

“Most critical is the initial barrier—this is the first point. During the phase of gathering and refining, if yin and yang fall into disorder, the sun and moon deviate from their course, and though the external fire is stirred and moves, the internal tally remains closed and unresponsive, all effort is wasted—this is the second point. If the firing regimen errs, the water and mercury are unstable, the source and flow muddied, and the medicinal substances impure, one labors in vain, harm outweighing benefit—this is the third point. Once the millet-pearl enters the crucible, it must be gently nurtured, the heart-monarch firmly guarded; should care be lacking, the fire may transform and the elixir be lost—this is the fourth point.

There are scholars who endure hardships, repeatedly face dangers, and are shaken in heart and gall. When the elixir is obtained and placed in the crucible, they must drive away all distractions, ensuring utmost clarity. If old worries linger and disturb the heart-monarch, this is called ‘washing the mind and cleansing thoughts’—the bath. Should attachment persist, lead and mercury may flee—this is the fifth point. When the ten-month gestation is complete and the embryo is transferred to a new crucible, if the yang-spirit is not securely guarded and is lightly released, it may wander astray upon exit, losing its abode and finding no return—this is the sixth point.

After the elixir is formed, one must discern truth from falsehood. If the work is incomplete and suddenly myriad marvels and extraordinary phenomena appear—even clairvoyance of past and future—these are all demonic obstructions, not true reality. One must not mistake them for the sacred power of one’s own elixir. Such illusions and deceptions arise when the Dao is near fruition, seeking to lure one into heterodox paths and disrupt true attainment. At this time, one must steadfastly uphold wisdom and preserve the integrity of the Real—this is the seventh point.

All these are the greatest concerns in vigilance. A single lapse in caution may not only prevent success but risk loss. As the Patriarch of Zhengyang said:
‘Having realized the formless, the eye rests in the heart,
One must nurture and protect the whole truth.
For a year, bathe and guard against peril,
Lest one sink into delusion and labor in vain.’

The *Sutra of Settled Contemplation* states: ‘Those who attain the Dao exhibit seven signs:
First, the mind easily attains stability, awakening to the leakage of dust.
Second, long-standing illnesses vanish, and body and mind become light and refreshed.
Third, celestial deficits are replenished, and life is restored to its original span.
Fourth, one extends life for thousands of years, becoming an immortal.
Fifth, one refines form into qi, becoming a perfected person.
Sixth, one refines qi into spirit, becoming a spirit-person.
Seventh, one refines spirit to merge with the Dao, becoming a supreme person.
In their power of discernment, each sign brings greater clarity, and upon attaining the Dao, wisdom becomes fully complete.’

The *Preliminary Discernments in Cultivating the Real* states: ‘This Dao cannot be attained by any but the truly resolute; it cannot be practiced by any but those of heaven-endowed, supreme intelligence. Even if one attains it, one must also possess deep accumulated virtue from ancestors, great merit and conduct of one’s own, and countless celestial affinities converging—only then can one proceed without obstruction, step by step, to accomplish this great matter. If ancestors lack virtue and one’s own karmic roots are shallow, though one may forge ahead with courage, one may be limited by unfinished affairs when fate is exhausted, or obstructed by demonic hindrances just as the work begins. Often, one has the method but lacks the resources, possesses the Dao but lacks the strength, and dies holding the Way. Yet even if one does not complete the Dao, one’s comings and goings are clear, vastly different from ordinary people. Some die halfway through the work, others before beginning. Those who die halfway will surely be born with innate knowledge in the next life; those who die before beginning will surely be born with the aspiration for the Dao.

Those with karmic roots awaken at a single reminder; those without, though pressed, will not heed. The determined, even if they study the Dao their whole lives without meeting a true teacher, upon rebirth will emerge knowing this great matter awaits, and by every means seek a teacher and inquire into the Dao. Without karmic roots, even if orphaned, widowed, impoverished, or beset by myriad hardships, they would rather perish in adversity than love this Dao.

Worldly people refuse to exhaust their minds in plumbing principles, lightly regard life and destiny, and before fulfilling human affairs, already aspire to immortality. They do not exert the slightest effort themselves but demand others’ treasures; the slightest dissatisfaction or hardship makes them retreat, abandoning the path halfway, attributing failure to lack of affinity. Such conduct—how can one enter the gate of the sages? No wonder they are misled by blind teachers and spend their lives in the den of ghosts. Consider: even a single skill or craft requires careful, prolonged study to master. How much more this great matter of life and death—does one expect to know it easily, to obtain it through casual learning? How foolish!’

The *Anthology of Transmitted Teachings* states: ‘All people in the world must die, yet those who love the Dao seek not to die and to live forever. All people dwell in the world, yet those who love the Dao seek to ascend as immortals and wander beyond things. All people live on earth, yet those who love the Dao seek to transcend the ordinary and enter the grotto-heavens. Thus, they endure hardship and embrace poverty, their minds roaming in purity and freedom, their traces hidden beyond springs, stones, mist, and clouds.

People of the world, before the neighbor’s rooster crows, rush out the door, chiding themselves for tardiness. When the street drums sound, they return home, regretting the earliness of their return. Greed and delusion never rest for a moment; vain thoughts only lament insufficiency. In the radiant sun and enchanting scenery, with flowers in full bloom, by waterside pavilions, in the refreshing breeze, during moonlit conversations, or snowbound drinking, they indulge without restraint in passion, squandering their limited emotions. Wealth and splendor merely adorn the dream of floating life; sorrows, attachments, and resentments merely sow the seeds of future lives.’”

As the song’s last notes fade, suffering arrives too soon. When fame and profit burn brightest, youthful beauty has already departed. Even if one turns the heart toward the Dao, illness has long taken hold. A sinking boat not yet crossed—who lacks the urge to seek rescue? A leaking roof patched again—how can one bear to abandon the will to repair?

“The years slip by, time fleeting and swift. Greed for wealth and attachment to possessions delude one into believing they will last ten thousand kalpas. Love for children and tenderness toward grandchildren foster the hope of eternal reunion. Only when grave illness clings to the body does the heart at last rest. Only when the final hour arrives does one let go. Even if the heart turns toward the Dao, how can one contend with old age and depleted qi? Spring snow and autumn blossoms endure but a moment; the glow of sunset and dawn’s moon cannot last.

“Those who follow the Dao rarely begin in youth. Yet if one cultivates in youth, the root and origin remain whole and firm, progress comes easily, and great accomplishment may be achieved in a thousand days. It is also rare to begin in middle age. Cultivating in middle age first requires restoration to wholeness, then the application of effort. At first, one reverses aging to regain youth; later, one transcends the ordinary to enter sagehood. If youth does not awaken and middle age does not reflect, one may turn to purity only after disaster strikes, or set the mind on the subtle and tranquil only after falling ill. For those who begin cultivation in old age, the first concern is preservation, then restoration, followed by minor attainment. Through accumulating merit in minor attainment, one reaches middle attainment; through accumulating merit in middle attainment, one reverses aging to regain youth, refines the form to abide in the world, and may then achieve great accomplishment.

“Those who follow the Dao first establish faith. Matters of affection, fame, and profit—all worldly concerns—must not alter their great resolve. Next, they cultivate bitter determination. Hardship and solitude, all realms of emptiness and stillness, must not sway their original intent. This bitter resolve must aim for great accomplishment, not merely settle for minor attainment.

“People of the world, failing to recognize the great Dao and unaware of heaven’s workings, often favor heterodox paths and delight in minor practices. The years slip by, no progress is seen, and in old age, illness and decline lead only to death. This causes later seekers of the Dao to dismiss immortality as empty talk and liberation as mere words. They hear but do not believe, or believe but lack bitter resolve. Faced with circumstances, they lose their will; swayed by external things, they abandon their purpose. Thus, they never escape the nine trials and ten demons.

“The nine trials are these: Before the great elixir is complete, one must endure cold and heat, requiring clothing through the four seasons—this is the first trial, the distress of clothing and food pressing upon the cultivator. Some bear heavy karmic debts from past lives, repaying them in this existence. Unable to abandon parents, they find no leisure, stealing moments of quiet amid busyness—this is the second trial, the constraint of elders. Loved ones—wife and children—cherished ones—father and mother—bind with chains of affection, adding daily to the cart of worldly sorrow, never ceasing. Even with a heart of purity, one cannot withstand the realm of worry—this is the third trial, the entanglement of love and attachment. Wealth spanning ten thousand households, rank reaching the three ducal offices—yet the deluded mind never rests, and greed for possessions only laments insufficiency—this is the fourth trial, the snare of fame and profit. In youth, one refuses to cultivate, and weak qi leads to illness. The stubborn heart never awakens, and karmic retribution brings disaster. One suffers in this life, serving as a warning to later generations—this is the fifth trial, the sudden onset of calamity. Some, urgently seeking a teacher for the great matter of life and death, fail to discern truth from falsehood. They may follow one skilled in clever speech or one with the appearance of an ancient Daoist. At first, they believe they have encountered immortals, only to later realize they have fallen prey to deceivers—this is the sixth trial, the constraint of blind teachers. Others, misled by blind teachers and reckless companions, point to side doors, chasing branches and leaves, never finding true accord. Minor practices and heterodox paths offer conflicting instructions. They do not know that the sun and moon, when they emerge, shine with great brightness, visible to all with eyes; that thunder, when it crashes, brings great shock, audible to all with ears. Yet they mistake the flickering light of fireflies and the croaking of frogs in a well for true illumination—this is the seventh trial, the divergence of opinions. Some change their minds morning and evening, forgetting their resolve whether sitting or standing. Delighted by fleeting pleasures, they grow weary of perseverance; beginning with diligence, they end in laziness—this is the eighth trial, the lack of firm resolve. In youth, fame and profit linger in the mind; in old age, children and grandchildren occupy the thoughts. Time is limited—do not say, ‘This year has passed, I will wait for the next.’ Affairs are endless—do not say, ‘Today is over, I will wait for tomorrow.’ If today does not guarantee tomorrow, how can old age reclaim youth?—this is the ninth trial, the wasting of years.

“The ten demons are of three kinds: first, those appearing outside the body in the present; second, those in dreams; third, those in inner vision. They manifest as sights of blossoming flowers, sounds of flutes and strings, tastes of sweet delicacies, scents of rare fragrances, emotions of ease, and spirits of exuberance. If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of the six thieves. They appear as jade towers and jeweled pavilions, painted beams and carved rafters, pearl curtains and embroidered drapes, orchid chambers and musk canopies, coral strewn across the floor, gold and jade filling the hall. If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of wealth. They appear as golden saddles and jeweled horses, stately canopies, lordships of ten thousand households, envoys with banners and flags, households filled with high officials, boots and tablets piled on the bed. If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of rank. They manifest as drifting mist and gentle sunlight, fierce winds and driving rain, thunder and lightning, clear songs and mournful weeping.”

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of the six emotions. They appear as kin in distress, family members afflicted by disaster, children stricken with illness, parents lost to death, brothers scattered apart, wives and children torn asunder.

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of love and attachment. They manifest as falling into cauldrons of fire, tumbling from high ridges, harmed by venomous creatures, wounded by poison, encountering violent gangs on the road, or meeting death through transgression.

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of calamity. They appear as the Ten Grounds in radiant light, the Three Pure Ones and the Jade Emperor, the Four Spirits and Six Luminaries, the Five Peaks and Eight Kings, their majestic rites and measured movements, soaring and circling in flight.

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of sages and worthies. They manifest as clouds of soldiers and horses, blades sharp as frost, weapons raised in combat, bows and arrows drawn taut, rushing to kill and harm, fierce and unstoppable.

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of blades and warfare. They appear as immortal maidens and jade women, arrayed in rows, pipes and songs swirling around, together performing the *Rainbow Skirt Dance*, pairs of red scrolls competing to present golden adornments.

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of female musicians. They manifest as many beauties, their alluring forms richly adorned, feasting by night in orchid pavilions, their jade bodies clad in light garments, their coquettish airs entangling men, vying to form couples.

If seen, do not acknowledge them—these are the demons of female beauty.

Those who uphold the Dao, when such visions appear before them, must neither acknowledge nor cling to them; thus, the heart will not waver, and the will shall not falter. In dreams, if these arise, neither acknowledge nor attach to them; thus, the spirit will not be deluded, and the hun-soul will not scatter. During inner contemplation, if such visions appear, do not follow the waves or chase the currents, nor mistake the thief for one’s child. Raise the true fire of samadhi to burn the body, and with a single wave, the host of demons will scatter.

The sequence of verification is as follows:
First, lewd and deviant desires cease entirely, and external conduct is cultivated in tandem. During the gathering of the elixir, the golden essence fills to abundance, the heart’s illusions naturally dissolve, and the yin ghosts are slain.

Next, the heart meridian surges upward, and sweet fluid fills the mouth.

Next, yin and yang clash and contend, and from time to time, the sound of wind and thunder is heard within the abdomen.

Next, the hun and po souls become unsettled, and dreams are filled with fearful and startling visions.

Next, the six bowels and four limbs may develop minor ailments, which heal without treatment.

Next, the cinnabar field grows warm at night, and one’s appearance becomes refined and elegant.

Next, when sitting in a dark room, the eyes emit a divine radiance.

Next, in dreams, one becomes bold and fearless, untouched by harm, uncheated by others, or as if embracing an infant and returning home.

Next, the golden gate and jade lock seal firmly, preventing nocturnal emissions and leakage.

Next, a clap of thunder resounds, the joints become unobstructed, and startled sweat flows from all four limbs.

Next, the jade fluid is simmered and rinsed, forming congealed butter.

Next, the numinous liquid thickens into a paste, one gradually comes to loathe the rank and foul, and it suffices for the mouth and belly.

Next, the sitting bones grow light, and the chamber of the spirit transforms. One’s steps keep pace with galloping horses, and movement becomes swift as flight.

Next, when facing external circumstances, the will remains steadfast, and all cravings and desires are severed.

Next, the true qi of people and things can be used to heal others’ illnesses.

Next, inner contemplation becomes bright and clear, free from obscurity.

Next, the pupils of the eyes become dark as lacquer, wrinkled faces smooth and rejuvenate, and gray hair regrows black; those who were once youthful retain their childlike countenance forever.

Next, the true qi gradually fills to abundance, and one seems perpetually sated, eating little. One may drink limitless wine without ever appearing drunk.

Next, the body becomes lustrous, and the spirit and qi grow refined and graceful. The sacred elixir produces flavor, the numinous liquid exudes fragrance, and true fragrance and wondrous tastes linger constantly between mouth and nose, perceptible even to others.

Next, the eyes can discern the finest details at a hundred paces.

Next, old scars and lingering blemishes naturally fade away. Tears, saliva, and sweat no longer appear.

Next, the fetal breath is complete and the qi is full, rendering food and drink unnecessary.

Next, the inner will becomes pure and lofty, merging with the Great Void. All mundane emotions and attachments sever themselves from the heart.

The nine worms are expelled from below, and the Three Corpses perish above. Next, the hun and po souls no longer wander, putting an end to dreams and sleep. The spirit’s radiance and essence remain vibrant, transcending day and night.

Next, the yang essence solidifies the body, and the divine palace stands firm, untouched by cold or heat. Next, life and death can no longer affect one, and through seated forgetfulness and inner contemplation, one roams the divine immortal realm of Huaxu—where towers of jade maidens and pavilions of splendor exist, far beyond the mortal world.

Next, when merit is complete and conduct perfected, hidden virtues manifest their rewards, and the Three Clarities secretly bestow the true talismans. The transformations of yin and yang allow one to foresee human affairs, and one’s actions foretell disasters before they strike.

Next, the dust and clutter of the world grow wearisome, and one seeks purity in stillness. The fetal immortal may then appear, and a body beyond the body emerges—this is the divine sage.

Next, the true qi becomes purely yang, and exhalations can dry external mercury. Next, the fetal immortal constantly yearns to ascend, and auspicious light fills the chamber. Next, in stillness, one often hears celestial music.

Next, even in the presence of ordinary people—no matter how wealthy or noble—they reek of foulness, for they are but mortal flesh and vulgar form. Next, one’s form naturally shifts, and the spirit’s radiance and immortal bearing resemble a jade tree, while a golden hue shines through the bones.

Next, wherever one goes, divine spirits come to pay homage, obeying commands as if by one’s own will. Next, in quiet contemplation, one sees purple mist filling the eyes, and beyond the neck, a golden light envelops the body.

Next, within the body, a fire dragon suddenly takes flight, or a dark crane ascends—this is the spirit shedding mortal bones to transcend into the sacred realm, called *transcendence*. After transcendence, colored clouds swirl, auspicious vapors abound, heaven rains rare blossoms, dark cranes soar in pairs, and wondrous fragrances fill the air. Jade maidens descend, bestowing celestial scriptures and purple decrees. Once the immortal crown and robes are complete, the regalia of authority—before and behind, left and right—are beyond counting. Escorted and guided, one returns to Penglai. First, in the Purple Palace, one pays homage to the Supreme Microcosmic Lord, where one’s origins, name, and merits are examined. Then, one is settled in the Three Isles as a *Perfected One* or *Immortal*.

*Baopuzi* states: “I once received the *Nine Elixirs* and the *Scripture of Gold and Silver Liquids* from Master Zheng, along with five scrolls of the *Central Scripture of the Yellow and White*. Master Zheng said that he and Master Zuo had once practiced the divine alchemical arts in the mountains of Lujiang, and all their trials succeeded.
